B0002XMF5S|4.0|I bought this album originally when it came out on cassette.  I was 16 at the time and just getting into Sabbath.  I was not aware then that this was actually Tony Iommi's first solo album and that label pressures slated it as Black Sabbath 'featuring' Tony Iommi.  At the time I will admit I was initially put off a little by the absence of Geezer Butler on bass but found it to be a pretty good effort with former Deep Purple singer-bassist Glen Hughes on vocals.<br /><br />Having recently purchased the album again on cd I can say it still stands up as a great misfit rock album that is perhaps a better Tony Iommi solo project than a transitional Sabbath album in hindsight.  The full on up tempo rocker 'In For The Kill' transitions into the powerful but ballad-esque 'No Stranger To Love' (the only single back then if memory serves me) and works better than it should for some reason oddly setting the mood for this album.<br /><br />They pick up the pace one more time with an in your face track 'Turn To Stone' and then Goeff Nicholls intros the rest of the album with a keyboard interlude 'Sphinx The Guardian' that begins a succession of plodding deliberate emotional tracks that take the rest of the album out through an overcast atmosphere provided by Iommi's characteristic heavy sound throughout.<br /><br />Seventh Star the title track pays homage to Sabbath's darker topics with middle eastern overtones.  Hughes delivers a soulful performance as usual always finding a voice for Iommi's vision.  'Danger Zone' makes a defiant plea not lost on youthful angst.  'Heart Like A Wheel' finds Hughes on a soul searching mission as Eric Singer and Dave 'The Beast?' Spitz roll through a bluesy backbeat for a long number that sets up Iommi for possibly his bluesiest exploration since Sabbath's early material on Warning - great stuff.  'Angry Heart' clarifies that this is an album about personal struggles and defiant willpower.  It breaks down to perhaps the most powerful track 'In Memory' which sort of stuck with me long after I lost the cassette years ago.<br /><br />Well worth it especially when you realize it's a Tony Iommi album.

B0060ZJ7BC|3.0|Being a lifelong A's fan I was fascinated exactly as to how Brad Pitt (oops, Brapi playing A's general manager Billy Beane) rebuilt the team in 2002 and got them back into the playoffs after losing three key players (including 2000 American League Most Valuable Player Jason Giambi) to free agency from the 2001 playoff team using a sabremetric (basically, a unique statistical method of evaluating player performance) strategy dubbed \\"Moneyball\\" by the author Michael Lewis who wrote the book of the same name that the movie is based on.<br /><br />Now, if this sounds too much like fantasy baseball, it kind of is. So, if you're not a sports fan who is interested in the behind-the-scenes machinations of how players are drafted, how trades are made and the inner workings of a baseball franchise, this is probably not the movie for you. There are long stretches where it seems like a lot of talking is going on and not much game action.<br /><br />The actual game footage spliced into the movie footage works great. It does capture the thrill of the A's record 20 consecutive win streak during that season. Yet, like the book, the movie overlooks two major factors that have nothing to do with this Moneyball approach. Firstly, is although ex-Giants shortstop Royce Clayton plays that season's AL MVP Miguel Tejada, a holdover from the 2001 team, he's barely a footnote in the story onscreen. Secondly, the A's starting pitching troika of Mike Mulder, Barry Zito and Tim Hudson were huge in the A's 2000-2003 playoff run. In fact, they were arguably one of the best starting pitching staffs in recent MLB history. You do see Hudson a bit in the movie, but the other two are not mentioned at all.<br /><br />Also, there was a lot of artistic license as Jeremy Giambi and Carlos Pena end up being traded on the same day for dramatic effect. The reality was Giambi was traded on May 22, 2002, and Pena was dealt July 5. Also, when Pitt discusses picking up submarining relief pitcher Chad Bradford at his meeting with the team's scouts prior to the 2002, I half expected some scout to tell Pitt that Bradford was already on the team. The A's acquired him in 2001.<br /><br />The fictional Ivy League educated stat wizard Peter Brand (played brilliantly low key by Jonah Hill) in the movies joins the A's in 2002 as Beane's righthand man. The real-life Paul DePodesta, whom the fictional Brand is based on, actually joined the A's in 1999.<br /><br />Also, the movie seems a bit unfair to A's manager Art Howe (played by Philip Seymour Hoffman). Howe led the A's to three straight playoff appearances. He may have been old school but did he really butt heads that much with Beane over lineup choices? Maybe he did but how about giving Howe a bit of credit for that 20-game winning streak as well. I'm pretty sure some of his managerial moves helped that streak along.<br /><br />OK, that's the nitpicking as an A's fan. Moneyball is still a terrific movie in showing how a small market team with a very low budget could compete with the likes of the deep pockets New York Yankees et al. The search for undervalued players was spot on and how Beane faced off against the old school style of scouting and evaluating players. The hero of the movie, Scott Hatteburg, a catcher turned first baseman, did prove to be of good value and a bargain to boot. Yet like the book, the movie makes too much of a case that the Moneyball way of finding undervalued assets led to the A's success in 2002. Moneyball was just part of the winning formula as having an MVP (Tejada) and those three great pitchers were major factors in the streak. Still it's pretty compelling to watch the streak unfold on the big screen.<br /><br />The extras have a terrific behind-the-scenes look at how the movie was made with Major League Baseball's full cooperation in helping the baseball scenes in actual MLB ballparks look as real as possible right down to the era's uniforms. There's also a good discussion on the whole Moneyballtheory with Billy Beane adding his own insight.<br /><br />Lastly, for trivia buffs, you have got to love the framed Clash posters in Beane's office. Who wouldn't love a baseball GM into punk rock from his era?theory with Billy Beane adding his own insight.<br /><br />Lastly, for trivia buffs, you have got to love the framed Clash posters in Beane's office. Who wouldn't love a baseball GM into punk rock from his era?

You know Mr. Thornton deserves to be seen in HD.  The first thing that struck me when I saw it is how it reminds me of Pride & Prejudice, yet being very different at the same time.  Biased preconception, culture clash, botched proposal - these are the similarities.  But the similarities quite end there.  North & South happens in a grittier, more tumultuous and progressive era.  People actually have important things to do and conflicts carry graver consequences.  Margaret's a less endearing character compared to Lizzy, but interesting nonetheless. And Mr. Thornton can rival with Mr. Darcy any day.  It's dark and moody, and yet it's incredibly romantic.  I also like it that the heroine does the rescuing at the end of the day.  Big big plus.<br />I've since read the novel as well and I can say the adaptation actually elevated Gaskell's writing.  No offense to Mrs. Gaskell, but I sure as heck did not formulate such vivid images as seen in the show when I read her words.  I sometimes felt swept away inside a scene due to the beautiful cinematography and haunting scores.  There are several little romantic moments from the book I wish I could see, but I think the script did a really good job of cutting and adding where it did to enhance the flow on screen (The genius line \\"Look back, look back at me\\" wasn't from the book).

Ana wants to work, she wants her own identity and living with a megalomaniac control freak is difficult, but she does an amenable job.<br /><br />This book is filled with one overwhelming problem after another. B0072GPQNG|4.0|On paper, this film must have appeared to be a tired contrivance-- so many cliches, so little to hold it together. Yet, somehow, after making films for 40 years, Spielberg continues to get better and better at what he does better than anybody else. One might say that the Spielberg film is a contradiction to the phrase \\"they don't make them like they used to.\\" Well, Spielberg does-- and in some ways better than anybody ever has. That isn't to say a movie like War Horse is going to offer new insights into the human condition, but it does breathe new life into the hopeful, sugar coated miracle stuff that Hollywood lives and breathes on.<br /><br />The images in this film are truly breathtaking. And, it should be said, they go beyond cliche and achieve poetry. It may be a kind of commercial poetry, but it is beautiful nonetheless. And though many of the situations feel fake (especially one with a sick child and her kindly grandfather), and some of the sequences just reek of manipulation, most of them work wonderfully. As much as I resisted, I teared up a couple of times. Cynics may disagree, but I think Spielberg has an extraordinary and authentic emotional sense-- and I choose to believe its genuine. The camera movement, the blocking-- the very conception of so many shots -- all function to communicate straight to the heart.<br /><br />Just contrast the simple emotion in this film to a much more famous-- but much more fraudulent film-- Forest Gump. Both films, of course, are wonderfully executed pieces of cinema magic. But while both are fairy tales, and both play upon our desire for wishes to come true, there's a big difference. In this film (like most of Spielberg's movies), the simple emotion comes from basic truths. People make fools of themselves, horses (and farms) get lost to the boss man and to war. Hard work means a lot but it doesn't always produce the desired result. But sometimes, you get back what you lose. Life isn't random, but it does spin off many colors. In Forest Gump, on the other hand, the meek not only inherit the earth, they also get to own their own conglomerate! And not only that-- those who work the hardest for change are shown up as fools. Thus, Forest Gump turns out to be based on a Big Lie -- the lie that Reagan sold to America-- that brains and creative thinking-- and daring-- were the road to death (Jennie) or disfigurement (Dan). Of course we know now that Reagan and his Big Lie actually destroyed the middle class-- and ruined our country. But Big Business keeps propping up the Reagan brand since his laissez-faire narrative made them rich at everyone else's expense.<br /><br />Spielberg's film, as mentioned before, is also a fairy tale. But it doesn't pretend (as does Forest Gump) to offer a new spin to a key decade in history. Gump is a travesty of the '60s-- and a slap in the face of real heroes like MLK and Robert Kennedy. War Horse's connection with WW1 is purely casual. This is not a film about a moment in time-- it is just a series of warm and fuzzy dreams that show us as we would like to be, and remind us that life is better when we are humble, kind, and a little brave. No-- we don't get Bubba Gump Shrimp company t-shirts or the Porsche that Bob Zemeckis probably drives around in. What we get-- and keep-- are our souls.<br /><br />So-- don't go see War Horse expecting a serious work of art (unless you accept Damien Hirst's definition of art as \\"anything done super well.\\") But do go if you want to experience Hollywood magic (for better or for worse) at its finest.

B002MQYOFW|5.0|First, let me say this is the first review I have ever written.  EVER.  But since lately I rely on other's reviews before buying just about anything, I thought I should share for the opportunity to some that may read it, how this book/series affected me.  For me, this is the first book that I have read in a long time that can balance on the fine line between being \\"deep\\" and trying so hard for depth that it's borderline ridiculous.  For the most part, I will enjoy a story but find it either very light and basic or the author is so desperate to create something \\"different\\" that it winds up not making sense.  (Should anyone be reading this and knows what I'm talking about, they are probably clicking to buy the book before even reading the rest of my review!)  What Collins has done in this book is the reason why I don't write-not many people can effectively achieve this.<br /><br />Summaries of the book can be found everywhere, so I won't go too far into it, but there is a line in the book that sticks with me.  Peeta says he is trying to find a way to show them he is more than just a piece in one of their games. It seems both this book and from what I have read into the rest of the trilogy the main part of the story centers around this idea, for at least the main characters, anyway.  What I found the most interesting was everything the book left unsaid.  If you are \\"with\\" the auther on her ideas you are given enough information to form your own answers about what is really going on.  As Katniss is trying to figure out the reasoning for everything that happens to her and the world of Panem, the reader is also in need of figuring out her perspective and sometimes, a perspective that she doesn't see.  Suzanne Collins, I can 100% appreciate where you were going with this book, and I'm with you!<br /><br />Now, if you are not able to pick up the message I have been talking about, you will still most likely enjoy the book very much, and not feel that you are missing out on anything.  As everyone seems to be saying, it is very well written with cliffhangers at the end of every chapter.  It keeps you moving forward and engaged.  Yes there is a bit of gore and there were a few times I was disappointed that the romance intensity did not pick up whatsoever, but I have to remember, the story is following the path the author has chosen, not what I would like to read.  So anything extra will just have to exist in my imagination!

1400069289|2.0|Once again the American publishing industry pushes out a too-long book that would have easily been a nine-page essay in the Atlantic monthly. Rather than an expert in the field, again we have a journalist who compiles a bunch of facts and stories and massages it into the standard creative-writing-program story-expand narrative framework. The book is big on promise, short on execution, and dishonest in exposition.<br /><br />The first part concerns some basic behavior psychology with a focus on the Cue-Routine-Reward cycle. This is somehow the miraculous discovery that will change your life. However, the book is so bereft of application for individual users, that you end of reading a bunch of nice stories about how other people changed, but still lack any methodology about how to apply it to yourself other than asking, \\"Hey, what are your cues?\\", \\"What reward does your habit give?\\".<br /><br />The author is exceedingly dishonest in trying to massage Paul O'Neil's turn-around of Alcoa into an example of habit forming. It is an excellent example of expertise in organizational behavior, however it is only tangentially related to habit formation. Habit formation of individuals is a neurological mechanism, habit formation is organizations is a multi-factor process dealing with organizational structure, power sharing, organizational goals, and individual management styles, thus the author is disingenuous when he links the two \\"habits\\" through equivocation.<br /><br />Overall, this was not worth the purchase price.

There's no explanation for the sudden change from \\"its not going to happen,\\" to the tournament actually happening.<br /><br />- The the main character is executed he has hold of essential information about the death of the king and the rise to the thrown of a child who is not actually the heir. I find it odd that upon finding out that he is going to be beheaded, the main characters does not divulge any of this information to the people in attendance and seemingly lets this HUGE secret die with him. Ultimately this makes the main character look like an idiot.<br /><br />There are numerous more holes, unexplained items and so on, that ultimately made Game of Thrones Season 1 less like a fantasy epic and more like a poorly written fantasy daytime soap opera (which can be fun in its own right, but surely not to be taken seriously in the way, of say, Lord of the Rings).

0385737955|2.0|James Dashner's THE MAZE RUNNER is one of the most frustrating novels I've ever slogged through.  It's not that the story isn't intriguing - it most certainly is!  The sixteen-year-old main character, known only as Thomas, finds himself in a pitch dark elevator (which is called a \\"lift\\" in the novel, as if it's taking place in England) without any memory of his previous life or how he got there.  When he's pulled out of the lift, he's in a strange place called the Glade, which is surrounded by massive stone walls and a mysterious maze inhabited by scary murderous mechanical creatures.  The hundred or so other boys trapped in the Glade have been trying for years to unravel the mystery of the maze so they can escape their prison, but so far no luck.<br /><br />Dashner's style here is pretty much a copy of the TV series LOST, which even fans have to admit was a pretty frustrating experience.  Everything in THE MAZE RUNNER is a mystery, and every time Thomas asks a logical question he's told to \\"shut up\\" or \\"you'll find out later\\" (or someone will be seconds from answering one of his questions when something interrupts him and you'll want to throw the book across the room!).  This may be a good way to stretch out the story, but it's an equally good way to alienate the reader.<br /><br />To add insult to injury, Dashner throws in a mysterious (of course!) girl, who shows up in the lift, the first girl to ever come to the Glade.  But . . . she's in a coma, so she can't explain why she's there . . . or what the mysterious message she delivers means.  I kept expecting the girl to play an integral role in the final act of the story, but she never does.  Even when she wakes up, she has little to say that's of any real value.  It was as if Dashner is following a formula here for YA fiction - a love interest is essential, so throw in a girl for Thomas.<br /><br />This brings me to the most frustrating part of all - as the first in yet another YA trilogy, THE MAZE RUNNER has no real ending.  Yes, a bunch of stuff is finally revealed (in a very annoying fashion - a character comes out of nowhere and just explains a few things), but none of it makes much sense.  And then Dashner drops in a two-page epilogue to set up the next book.  So the reader is left staring at the words \\"END OF BOOK ONE\\" with her mouth hanging open.  End of book one????  Books in a trilogy have to work on their own, even if they encourage readers to buy the next installment.  This one doesn't do that at all.  It just presents a ton of mysteries but offers little that makes sense in way of explaining them.  And I had more questions by the end of the book than I did as I was reading it . . . and not in a good way.  There were just so many loose ends, things that didn't jive, and red herrings dropped in for no apparent purpose.<br /><br />I like dystopian thrillers, and Dashner has envisioned a story that had the potential to be as exciting and satisfying as ENDER'S GAME (with which it has a lot in common).  But THE MAZE RUNNER doesn't live up to that potential.  The characters are thinly drawn, the plot is overrun with both deus ex machina moments and predictable developments, and there is nothing satisfying about the \\"ending.\\" Read this one at your own risk.real ending. B007G5K066|5.0|Let me preface this review with the caveat that I have not read John le Carre's novel but I have seen and rather enjoyed the classic BBC television adaptation of \\"Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy\\" starring Alec Guinness as George Smiley.<br /><br />It should go without saying that this post-millennial theatrical adaptation is a different animal. Directed by Tomas Alfredson, whose previous feature \\"Let the Right One In\\" was rightly heralded as an instant classic, has produced with the considerable aid of his screenwriters Bridget O'Connor and Peter Straughan an extremely stylish, precise, and condensed version of the story. The plot, layered not only in events and characterizations but also in themes, is complicated but not impenetrable; new information is constantly revealed to the audience as the film unfolds, which makes complaints that this film is \\"boring\\" all the more baffling to this reviewer. Attentive viewers should have no trouble following the film.<br /><br />Cold War paranoia infests every frame. Every detail of the film feels calibrated, from the angles of shots to the drab colors of the sets to the postures of the actors, each of whom is superb in their roles: Gary Oldman portrays a quieter, more detached Smiley than Guinness, Mark Strong personifies the sadness of Jim Prideaux, and Tom Hardy makes a surprisingly vulnerable Ricky Tarr.<br /><br />If I were to boil down the key difference between this film and the earlier BBC version (other than, of course, the length of time in which to tell the story), I would have to say atmosphere. Both are extremely well-crafted, well-written, and well-acted, but it was only in this film version where I could palpably feel the tension of the scenario.<br /><br />This Blu-ray features a fine, film-like transfer. Extra features are not overly abundant, consisting of an audio commentary from Alfredson and Oldman, a couple of deleted scenes, and filmmaker interviews. The most interesting extra for me personally was the interview with le Carre himself where the author discusses his background and delves much deeper than the usual fluff piece.<br /><br />The story of \\"Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy\\" is an espionage classic, and this latest film adaptation is a fine example of the genre and worthy of standing alongside the BBC version and other classics in the fold. Highly recommended.

B005F6NA56|5.0|That a mainstream pop singer-songwriter can be this talented ought to be some sort of crime. Feist manages childlike sincerity, wonder, and vulnerability concurrently with maturity, fiendish songwriting genius, and summative command of all that she presents herself to be. I think I hate her.<br /><br />Part of what sets this artist apart from many others is her acute sensitivity to texture. Her guitar playing, objectively evaluated, cannot be said to be virtuosic, and yet she's able to transform her mere technical competence into textural genius; every note, every acoustic string buzz-out seems deliberate, and the results usually lead to a composition that may be simple in theory, but the cascade of sounds seem to belie the theory. There's too much going on to dismiss this lady as a pop singer bent on catchy melodies, as much of the music on here is definitely not meant for radio play.<br /><br />Considering all of this, the greatest triumph on this record for this reviewer's money is definitely \\"Caught a Long Wind.\\" The song begins with interwoven sounds and takes a few subtly different twists and turns from there, at one point held together with nothing but a clap. An organic Kate Bush influence is undeniable, but the piece is squarely Feist's own. Her ability to take a twee subject and make it into an intriguing listen is evidenced by lyrics like the cute-clever \\"Good morning bird/good nightingale\\" and the deeper \\"The current tells of what the wave withheld.\\"<br /><br />As a die-hard progressive rock fan, I suppose it's asking too much in 2012 for a mainstream songwriter to undertake a concept album project. Fair enough, but she could do it with wondrous results. At least it's time for some official live material. Either one would be welcomed.

B0076NAWWA|4.0|Well, when I read some Mike Portnoy interviews late last year describing this line up and effort, I thought \\"hmmmm...being a semi-fan of Spock's Beard, Transatlantic, and even Steve Morse, this should be interesting.\\"<br /><br />And it is.  It's catchy.  Seriously, within one or two listens I had some melodies sticking in my head, and I was unconsciously humming to a couple of tunes.  Then after a fourth or fifth listen I was starting to understand that \\"instantly catchy\\" doesn't necessarily mean \\"enduring\\".  Yes, there's a \\"Coldplay\\" vibe in a few of the pop-rock tunes, and I don't fault these guys for trying something different, that's for sure.  And there are some Coldplay songs I actually like, so that's not necessarily a show-stopper either.  I guess as much as I tried to manage my expectations on this, I couldn't shake the feeling of slight disappointment with it as well.  Given the pool of talent on this, you almost can't avoid having high expectations, and even knowing the band was aiming for more of a pop-rock record, I guess I was hoping/expecting something that would push the boundaries of pop-rock more than sounding like Coldplay or something off a \\"Dawson Creek\\" soundtrack.<br /><br />Positives:<br />- The vocalist.  Nice voice and solid delivery of the songs.  I've moaned about Neil Morse' voice in other reviews.  It's not horrible, but it's not a great \\"lead voice\\".  Some folks have oboe-voices (thin and reedy) and others have full-bodied baritone sax voices that are nice instruments all by themselves (not unlike Greg Lake or John Wetton in their prime).  This Casey-guy from the band \\"Alfa-Rev\\" (sp?) has a very solid voice, and MUCH better voice than Neil Morse.  The stark difference was VERY obvious to me the one or two songs were Neil takes a lead line or two while Casey is doing most the singing<br />- There are some very nice hard-rocking, proggy jams in a few of the songs.  Steve Morse blows away a few fine solos, and that, tied with the energy in some of the music makes for an enjoyable listen.  And bass, drums, and keys deliver very nicely as well, and as expected, the playing is very tight<br />- Some well-written, likeable songs<br />- I actually liked the variety of styles even though the strength of the songwriting wasn't always consistent<br /><br />Negatives:<br />- When Neil sings I wish he wouldn't (I even prefer Mike Portnoy's voice)...though I admit it's a negative mostly in direct comparison to the better lead vocalist, Casey-what's-his-name.  And Neil really doesn't sing much on this fortunately (in his defense, I think he's a fine backing and harmonizing vocalist)<br />- That Coldplay/Dawson-Creek soundtrack vibe.  Flying Colors isn't the only supposedly \\"prog\\" outfit that borders on this sound (there are a few newer post/prog bands that are sounding more pop than the prog or post rock they profess to be)<br />- Not enough cool jams and player indulgence.  I know that wasn't the aim of the band for this record, but STILL I found myself missing it<br /><br />So, why did I give is a 4?  Well, I really did like it for the most part.  Nothing on it sucks...some of it is just \\"ho-hum\\" and didn't seem to live up to its potential. B006HGXGWU|5.0|Jeff Nichols' new film, \\"Take Shelter\\" is a marvelous example of what happens when a group of talented people come together to tell a smart story and make a good film, despite the fact that few will see it and even fewer will appreciate it. I already know not everyone likes this movie, there are other comments right here that in no uncertain terms say so, some even yell so, this is, of course, because the movie is something of a challenge for many viewers. It plays out as a mystery, we've already lost many people here brought up on Hollywood effects driven blockbusters that explains it all in the trailers, a psychological drama and a character study, it is what is missing so often today at the multiplex or new on Netflix, an adult thriller aimed at a willing audience.<br /><br />Written and directed by Jeff Nichols, a relative newcomer with only one other film under his belt, [[ASIN:B0016MJ6I8 Shotgun Stories]] which played at Roger Ebert's Overlooked Film Festival a few years back, Nichols writes a compelling script that translates into a gripping movie where things may not be what they seem, or as we the audience begin to care, we fear that they are what they seem and we don't like what we see. This works largely because of the fine acting of Michael Shannon, a great actor giving one of his greatest performances as the central character, Curtis. Curtis is a working class midwesterner who as the movie begins is plagued by a continuing series of nightmares about a coming near apocalyptic storm. Jessica Chastain is equally good, just how many good performances did she give in 2011, as Samantha, Curtis' loving wife, she gives the character the proper empathy to emote reactions from the viewer, we can see through her that sometimes being the witness can be the harder thing to have to be.<br /><br />The only major flaw I can find with the movie is that it is perhaps drawn out a little bit too much. There are those who will, and do find the leisurely way the film unfoldsto be boring, I think a lot of these people are too caught up in the modern era of instant gratification. When one is brought up on video games and movies based on such or easily transformed to the medium, expecting patience is difficult, if not all together futile. However, I do think that a small amount of tightening up in the center of the film might have made it's flow a little more gripping.<br /><br />The ending is the kind that I normally hate in a movie like this, it is ambiguous, I don't have a problem with ambiguity, but the way it is played sometimes can test a viewer's tolerance. Here though, I don't believe that to be the case. The ending seems to me to be the exact right conclusion, and it knows when to stop. I had a strong emotional reaction to this film, in no small part due to the near brilliance of the movie's final ten minutes or so.<br /><br />For the right audience, this is one of the best films of 2011. It is also the best thriller in a long time, period. I hope beyond all hope it finds a good audience, critical reaction has been up to this point, mostly favorable. In fact, many were surprised by Shannon's omission from the Oscar race. I say see it and I look forward to future films from this talent new filmmaker.<br /><br />Check out my reviews at moviezonemagazine.com

It is a story mired in filth and obscenity and shines the light on the worst conditions of human experience and offers them up as plot lines, dialogue and personal, social and political interactions.<br /><br />For those that want a fantasy/sci-fi story, this series is not for you.  Although there are dragons, dire wolves and other various fantasy elements thrown into the storyline, they are secondary to what is taking place; the fantasy elements are obligatory color for an otherwise drab world.  Through five novels, Martin's audience is treated to what I would like to call a dark, sad attempt at a Harlequin romance novel instead of an engaging fantasy story; however, that would insult the Harlequin novel genre, so the audience is left to call it what it is: trash.<br /><br />For plot lines, we have incest, rape, murder, extremely gratuitous violence, deception, betrayal and, to whet the appetite even further, more rape.  The actions of the varied protagonists represent the lowest forms of human behavior.  I cannot even begin to fathom Martin's impetus to start or even want to finish this series; perhaps that is why it took six years to get the fifth offering published, he could not understand it, either.<br /><br />One of the reviews said that the characters are engaging.  I call foul on that one.  The characters might have a chance to be engaging if: 1) They stuck around long enough to engage you; 2) The POV did not change so often; and 3) They were believable.  It seems as if Martin deliberately destroys a character just to prove he can do it, especially if that character offers balance, decency and justice in his world.<br /><br />Overall, this seemed to be an attempt to write the fantasy equivalent of a dark Harlequin novel, at which it failed, due to its utter lack of redeeming virtues; the characters are flat, throwaway chess pieces whose only draw is that they die violently, or are rendered ineffective in some horrific manner or are caught up in the basest of human emotions; and the storytelling is something I expect from an average sophomore English student in college, not an established writer.  If I could give the series a half a star, I would.  I honestly do not understand how anybody in their right mind could compare Martin to Tolkien based on this series; what an insult to Tolkien and his legacy.<br /><br />If this is the latest and best that George R. R. Martin has to offer, I suggest a career change, perhaps the editors of the dark and trashy magazine, Heavy Metal, need a new writer...pieces whose only draw is that they die violently, or are rendered ineffective in some horrific manner or are caught up in the basest of human emotions; and the storytelling is something I expect from an average sophomore English student in college, not an established writer. 0446556750|4.0|It's one of the golden rules of mystery fiction:  the person who looks guilty at the beginning of the book is rarely the actual culprit.  Of course, Michael Connelly - one of the premier mystery writers working today - is good enough to break the rules when necessary.  So is Jason Jessup guilty of killing a young girl?  Maybe.  Even if so, can he be found guilty in court?<br /><br />That is the task of series character Mickey Haller, the so-called Lincoln Lawyer (who generally uses his car as his office).  Jessup was originally convicted in the 1980s, but after he is granted a retrial due to new DNA evidence, Haller is asked to be an independent prosecutor on the retrial.  Haller is normally a defense attorney, but he agrees under certain conditions:  his ex-wife Maggie - who is a prosecutor - gets to be his second chair, and his lead investigator gets to be Harry Bosch (Connelly's principal series character).<br /><br />While it seems likely that Jessup is actually guilty, proving it a quarter-century after the last trial will be difficult.  Bosch is an astute detective and can turn up new evidence; Haller is good at working trials.  The story is essentially a courtroom thriller, though like most such stories, it takes a while to get to the actual trial.<br /><br />The book follows Bosch and Haller in alternate chapters.  As with other Haller stories, the chapters about Mickey are told in the first person; the Bosch chapters are told with a third person point-of-view (which is the case with most Bosch novels).  Although they occupy around equal space in the story, the different narrative styles make this more of a Haller book; the reader will more closely identify with him.<br /><br />For most writers, this would be five-star material, but I hold Connelly to a higher standard.  Though most of the book is good, it falters toward the end, though in ways I can't describe without spoilers.  This is not Connelly's best book, but it will be good enough for most mystery fans.

0571202284|5.0|Most people have learned the adage, \\"Learn the rules, then you can break them\\" but when it comes to art, what are the rules? And when it comes to commercial art with subjective tastes and evolving styles, again I ask, what are the rules?<br /><br />This book begins, logically, with the history of animation. If you don't know how we got to where we are today it's really easy to find yourself reinventing the wheel, and Richard Williams is big on avoiding reinventing wheels. He supports efficiency and economy of effort but wisely points out that sometimes the only shortcut is simply elbow grease. Software sellers rarely suggest this is necessary. It may seem to some that he's starting slow and waxing poetic about days long gone but he's actually helping animators consider a change audiences don't seem to catch.<br /><br />Pure animation, and we're not talking story here, is a visual medium that is expressed through drawing. Drawing can take many forms but the real jumping off point is drawing from what we know. What do we know? We know the physical world around us. A new animator might argue that we aren't photographers and that we aren't trying to replicate what we see. William's argues that until we understand what we see and can express that perfectly we will never be able to pull off into more imaginative work convincingly because our skill level is lacking. This point is repeated throughout the book for a reason: it's sensible. We may have computers to create the in-betweens, but if we don't know how an inbetween should behave we really don't know what to ask of our assistants, be they computers or hired hands. Williams is an interesting study regarding this because he began his career as many amateur animators might. He jumped in head first with complete disdain for the 'old school' animation and got lucky enough to build a career from it. This worked well for awhile until he really had to grow and realized the 'old school' was a very disciplined bunch, whose talents could rival or subjugate the best of today. Seeing that the men of the old school (mainly Disney talent) were fading away he hired a few to work for him and, in turn, soak up everything they have to offer. And here it is.<br /><br />We get lessons on masses, perspective, movement, timing, spacing, production methods, walks, runs, jumps, flexibility, weight, anticipation, takes, accents, wave/whip action,  dialogue, physical expression, animal action, camera movement, character blocking and directing. Lots of pages, lots of drawings, plenty of straight forward examples.<br /><br />There is no other animation book that covers so much, so thoroughly, so clearly in such a logical, helpful sequence. This is definitely a book all animator's must have.<br /><br />of course there are some who will pick this up and think, 'All I want to do is South Park type stuff'. William's addresses that attitude in a poem he posted for all his workers to see as he headed 'Who Framed Roger Rabbit'.<br /><br />\\"Epitaph of an Unfortunate Artist\\"<br />He found a formula<br />for drawing<br />comic rabbits.<br />This formula for<br />drawing comic<br />rabbits paid.<br />So in the end he<br />could not<br />change the<br />tragic habits<br />This formula for<br />drawing comic rabbits made.<br /><br />If you're trying to build an animation career I can't think of a better book to start with.

B003MC5N28|4.0|Before there was The Hunger Games, or the world of new Young Adult genre selections we often see today with beautiful and appealing covers that enthralls our imagination and tends to our emotions, there was The Giver. It has been a book of its own caliber. One that tends to the human spirit and intellect with a sophisticated approach to society and the individual. If you read this book in childhood, it will stay with you for the rest of your life. If you read this book in adulthood, it will blow your whole view of life wide open.<br /><br />As one of the Newbery Medal Award winners for the most oustanding contribution to children's literature, the author deserves some mention. Lois Lowry is absolutely one of those writers of simple genius, a seemingly effortless genius. This is what she says about the book (from Random House Website).<br /><br />\\"I will say that the whole concept of memory is one that interests me a great deal. I'm not sure why that is, but I've always been fascinated by the thought of what memory is and what it does and how it works and what we learn from it. And so I think probably that interest of my own and that particular subject was the origin, one of many, of The Giver.\\"<br /><br />A seemingly utopian society for the purpose of the greater good. Every year is significantly structured to promote the appropriate and standardized growth of each child. At the age of twelve, all children are given their 'adult' places/careers/duties in society. They are taught that questioning and having existential wonderings are 'stirrings,' and should be treated by the use of pills. Their lives are built around the concept of conformation. Conform or be 'appealed.' Mostly everything becomes an automatic and possibly even meaningless gesture.<br /><br />This book is about Jonas's journey through this society. All events in the book matter, and every page is significant to the transformation of the story. At twelve, Jonas is chosen with the responsibility of being the new Receiver of Memory. How does one bear the burden of memory of the whole human experience? The agony of war. Disease. Loss and pain. Even the heights of human existence: human acheivement, freedom, music, color, and the power of love. He, alone, keeps these memories on behalf of his community. He isn't to share them with a single person. The reason being that no-one, supposedly, wants to experience the whole range of human emotion. Additionally, this limitation on the society as a whole means that if people cannot remember things then they cannot remember the pain that comes with life as well, holding people in ignorant bliss.<br /><br />The ending felt much like being vaguely abandoned. There is a sense of ambiguity about the ending when you get to it. The Giver is the first in the series of three book, the second being Gathering Blue and the third being The Messenger. Just with any series, the placement of such a vaguely placed ending probably is meant to lead us into the next books in the series for us to find out more. Here is a word from the author about the end, for she knows best why that was accomplished in that way, and it will probably put things into perspective for you, the read, as to how to approach the end the way Lois Lowry might have intended it.<br /><br />\\"Many kids want a more specific ending to The Giver. Some write, or ask me when they see me, to spell it out exactly. And I don't do that. And the reason is because The Giver is many things to many different people. People bring to it their own complicated sense of beliefs and hopes and dreams and fears and all of that. So I don't want to put my own feelings into it, my own beliefs, and ruin that for people who create their own endings in their minds.\\"<br /><br />I have my own personal relationship to this book, and it has become a strong part of me. It's made its mark on me. It's truly one of those book you have to read in your lifetime. I absolutely loved it. I will be coming back to it throughout life, it's just one of those timeless books for both children and adults.

B005Q5OLC2|5.0|This book delights at many levels. For one who grew up in the south east of Ireland in the 1950-60s it transported me back to many familiar sights, sounds, smells and moments - with uncanny accuracy. The delectation is in the detail and Colm T&oacute;ib&iacute;n has his details spot on. Mrs Kelly's shop, the Sunday night dance, early Mass, the Courtown Hotel, Curracloe and Ballyconnigar strand all evoke vivid memories. The petty snobbery, importance of appearances, and the social catastrophe of mass forced emigration are captured with a light touch, without anything actually being said about them.<br /><br />Indeed it is the absence of saying anything about anything important that is probably the salient impression I take from the novel. Real issues are never discussed openly by the main characters or sometimes not at all. We Irish are masters at skirting the subject, dropping hints, making snide comments, raising an eyebrow to deliver a message but rarely are we ones for coming out in the open, addressing an issue up-front. We are certainly better than we were but the old ways of buttoned-up silent emotions that T&oacute;ib&iacute;n lays out brilliantly remains our default stance.<br /><br />What is exceptional about the main character Eil&iacute;s Lacey is that she is endowed with heightened intuition for a young woman of her age and time. She can see into and beyond what people say to what they are thinking and feeling and her capacity for discernment is breathtaking. Her poise and manner set her apart but her inability to freely express her own inner emotions eventually is her undoing.<br /><br />Denial runs through these pages as a leitmotif. Eil&iacute;s's recently dead father is hardly mentioned, the harrowing feelings of everyone concerned at her departure for America (including her own) and other later events are never expressed, tears are shed in private, major issues are avoided or left to simmer, letters are written without crucial content, joy, love, ecstasy are barely acknowledged. T&oacute;ib&iacute;n has illustrated an innate Irish ethnic trait in the most subtle yet damming way - by slowly and painstakingly unearthing its essence while never actually naming it. He learned his manners well in Enniscorthy.<br /><br />The other themes of the book for me are emigration and women. The experience of a houseful of Irish emigrants in Brooklyn is brilliantly portrayed but it could be mirrored by similar groups in any part of England at the time, except for the particular constraints imposed by the distance from home. Lack of education meant limited opportunity and the Irish tended to huddle together for support and comfort. The beneficent influence of the Catholic Church gets its due as a bulwark of strength in a somewhat hostile environment. What is absent is any sense of frustration, anger or betrayal that this should be the lot of these young people - deserted by a nation pledged to cherish them. And that too is authentic. It is only in recent times that Irish people have moved beyond mute acceptance of forced mass emigration and started to ask the why questions.<br /><br />Apart from Fr Flood who helps Eil&iacute;s in Brooklyn there is hardly another major male character. T&oacute;ib&iacute;n has a sub-plot here as well I suspect. In the official Ireland of government, news, church, and commerce it was only men who were seen or heard. Women were confined behind the lace curtains but that did not diminish their power exercised in ways that men neither understood nor felt. This is a hymn of praise to Irish women of that era who did not merely suffer in silence but rose above it to act with dignity, sense, courage and humility as beautifully revealed in the portraits of Eil&iacute;s, her mother and her sister Rose. For accuracy sake the narrow mindedness and cruelty of other women is there too in Mrs Kelly and Mrs Kehoe but their parts merely serve to highlight the heroism of the Lacey women. Like many mothers and daughters of their time duty was what counted - knowing it and doing it come what may--and never letting the side down.<br /><br />The book left me with much to remember and much to regret. As I wallowed in the nostalgia T&oacute;ib&iacute;n craftily created I began to become uneasy and a sense of foreboding accompanied every page turn. As Eil&iacute;s got happier I become more alert, more aware of impending doom. In that older Ireland joy was not just fleeting it was distrusted and discouraged. \\"You'll soon get your comeuppance me boyo!\\" And just on cue T&oacute;ib&iacute;n brought me down to earth--but bless him--he didn't leave me totally devastated at the end.e what may--and never letting the side down.<br /><br />The book left me with much to remember and much to regret. As I wallowed in the nostalgia Tibn craftily created I began to become uneasy and a sense of foreboding accompanied every page turn. As Eils got happier I become more alert, more aware of impending doom. In that older Ireland joy was not just fleeting it was distrusted and discouraged. \\"You'll soon get your comeuppance me boyo!\\" And just on cue Tibn brought me down to earth--but bless him--he didn't leave me totally devastated at the end.

0307959856|3.0|To begin, I should specify that this was my first novel by P. D. James and my first Pride & Prejudice sequel, so I think my feelings are unbiased by comparisons. Whether this is to the detriment or to the benefit of the novel, I cannot say. I should also add that I was prepared to like this book--I enjoy mysteries, I've heard good things about James, and, most important, the book was a gift from my father. Despite all this, I was somewhat disappointed.<br /><br />First, there is nothing to criticize in James's style. Although it lacked the sparkle and the exact cadence of Austen's writing, each sentence was interesting and each detail carefully chosen. I also greatly enjoyed Part I of the book, in which James fills in some of the gaps of the original. It's clear that she has spent a lifetime contemplating P&P, and her thoughts are honest and nuanced.<br /><br />However, in my opinion, the plot of the novel suffered from several problematic and confusing gaffes. First, although this novel is perfectly set up--by nature of it taking place at Pemberley the night before a ball (voil&agrave;, a country house and an assortment of out-of-town, independently wealthy guests)--to be an English country house mystery (or at least to earn some nods and winks to the genre) it neglects almost every convention. There is no detective character (professional, amateur, or otherwise), which means that the important details came to light only incidentally. In that way, the structure of the story more closely resembles a newspaper article than a murder mystery.<br /><br />Second, I think that James erred in choosing to off the least important or sympathetic character in the novel. I simply did not care who the murderer was, nor am I convinced that any of other the characters did either. The whole affair lacked a sense of intrigue or urgency, and the novel--left totally devoid of suspense--suffered accordingly.  Furthermore, no indications of the murder appeared earlier in the novel, robbing the reader of the anticipated sense of co-sleuthing. As opposed to everyone being under suspicion, no one was, and the only reason I guessed the murderer (POSSIBLE SPOILER ALERT) is that I felt one of the characters entirely superfluous and deduced that his only conceivable purpose was to be revealed as the murderer at the end of the novel.<br /><br />An incidental complaint is that the novel's structure seemed poorly edited. Certain details or events were recounted or narrated time and time again by different characters. Instead of simply recounting that Darcy told Elizabeth what had happened (which the reader already knew), Darcy would explain the whole thing to Elizabeth, and then explain the whole thing again to Colonel Fitzwilliam, and possibly explain the whole thing again to any one else who happened to turn up.<br /><br />Finally, and perhaps most fatally, Elizabeth's character lacks every trace of sardonic wit so characteristic of her predecessor. Her dialogue is limited and, when present, straightforward.<br /><br />It could be argued that James made all of these choices deliberately and with a higher aim in mind. Regardless, the effect was missed. In the end, I would say that the book was worth the time spent reading it, but only barely.e reader of the anticipated sense of co-sleuthing. B000F8O4DQ|5.0|Electric Light Orchestra  \\"A New World Record\\"  #5  (1976).<br /><br />Electric Light Orchestra was on a roll, after they struck platinum in 1975 for \\"Face The Music\\".  Jeff Lynne, Bev Bevan, Richard Tandy and Kelly Groucutt went back to Munich, Germany to record the follow up \\"A New World Record\\".  This album would go on to sell over 5 million copies.  And oh what an album this is, not a weak track in sight.  You get the old Move hard rocker \\"Do Ya\\", which hit #96 in 1971, they would hit #24 this time around, this is a more polished piece, but the best version maybe on their box set \\"Flashback\\".  \\"Tightrope\\", with it's swirling strings, is a heavy pop-rocker.  \\"Rockaria\\", the operatic rocker, good slide guitar by Jeff Lynne.  \\"Shangri-la\\", lush orchestrated number with great drumming by Bev Bevan, nice Beatle name dropping.  \\"Livin' Thing\\", hit #13, a very good song.  \\"Telephone Line\\", #7, their highest charting hit up to this point, very John Lennon sounding.  \\"Mission (A World Record)\\", Richard Tandy shines here, one of Lynne's best vocals.  \\"So Fine\\", nice little pop-rockeer, with nimbling bass by Kelly Groucutt, this pre-dated \\"Discovery\\" by 3 years.  The bonus material has the 50's rave-up \\"Surrender\\".  The other stuff is passable, but not that interesting.  One of ELO's all-time best records.
